<div id="review_386604499" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/386604499" />
    <a title="Andrea" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3727861-andrea"><img alt="Andrea" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442528074p2/3727861.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/386604499?book_show_action=true">Aug 07, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andrea" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andrea" href="/user/show/3727861-andrea">Andrea</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3727861-andrea?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3727861-andrea?shelf=1900s-1950s-era">1900s-1950s-era</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3727861-andrea?shelf=spaceships">spaceships</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3727861-andrea?shelf=space-adventure">space-adventure</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer386604499" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9516964366265330388">There's considerable charm in the opening of this book, and it's likely to entertain kids and adults as the story follows small-town boy Kip on his quest to reach the moon. Kip has little money, few connections, and the odds seem against him, but his methodical determination sees him entering competitions, and then repairing a decommissioned space suit. This section of HSS-WT is, I think, the best part of the story. Who can't connect with the idea of having big dreams and scant chance to fulfil </span>
  <span id="freeText9516964366265330388" style="display:none">There's considerable charm in the opening of this book, and it's likely to entertain kids and adults as the story follows small-town boy Kip on his quest to reach the moon. Kip has little money, few connections, and the odds seem against him, but his methodical determination sees him entering competitions, and then repairing a decommissioned space suit. This section of HSS-WT is, I think, the best part of the story. Who can't connect with the idea of having big dreams and scant chance to fulfil them? The story is set in the near-future, with a space station and moon base, but faces all the limitations which make star travel both expensive and unlikely. Heinlein nicely balances scientific discussion with story as Kip repairs his space suit, and leaves the reader thinking: these are things worth knowing.<br /><br />The story then kick-starts into a fast-paced and dramatic space adventure, which is still entertaining, though a rather different sort of story, and leads up to a not uncommon SF plotline <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[that of aliens judging humanity worthy – or not – of living <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. It's a satisfying enough ending.<br /><br />Kip is the usual sort of Heinlein hero – intelligent, modest, honest, and stuffed full of lessons on the importance of self-reliance (though occasionally inclined to jump to conclusions, not to mention making the worst "save the race" speech ever). He encounters Peewee, a precocious eleven year-old girl genius, described by her father as "an amoral little wretch". Peewee was "poking around where I shouldn't, doing things they told us not to do" and got caught up in something important. Though probably wearying to actually be around, she's definitely a highlight of the book.<br /><br />It's not uncommon for Heinlein to write exceptional women (ie. women who are vastly superior to other women, 99.99% of whom are complete idiots). Among his alien races, the females are, as often as not, depicted as active movers in their societies – leaders and scientists. [Though even the alien female in this book – a kind of intergalactic policewoman – is given the name "Mother Thing", and is valued primarily for showering everyone she comes across with lashings of mother love.]<br /><br />Among human women, there are various varieties of idiots, and exceptional women who grow up to marry exceptional men, whereupon their brains dribble out of their ears.<br /><br />There are three human females and one alien female given time in this book. Kip and Peewee's fathers are genius scientists. Their mothers are the people who make dinner, say things like "Don't bother your father", exclaim in disbelief at adventures, or are simply quietly supportive. Kip spends a lot of time discussing his father's intelligence, eccentricity, and the powerful role he played in shaping Kip. His mother is described thus:<br /><br /><i>I have talked more about my father, but that doesn't mean that Mother is less important – just different. Dad is active. Mother is passive ; Dad talks, Mother doesn't. But if she died, Dad would wither like an uprooted tree. She makes our world.</i><br /><br />Now, on a general level this is no big deal – that's just his Mother's personality. Until you get to the end of the book and discover that Kip's Mother – that passive, silent woman who has been shown doing nothing much beyond 'mothering' – was once his genius mathematician father's star pupil.<br /><br />Exceptional women in Heinlein's books all too often exist to provide a suitable mate for Heinlein's exceptional men. It's clearly indicated in the text that Peewee is likely to grow up and marry Kip and one shudders to think of Peewee – precocious, amoral, adventurous child! – condemned to have her brain dribble out of her ear.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9516964366265330388" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Kateblue" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/76979971-kateblue"><img alt="Kateblue" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1600299387p2/76979971.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

<div id="review_811503800" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/811503800" />
    <a title="Danny Tyran" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12973215-danny-tyran"><img alt="Danny Tyran" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1515776302p2/12973215.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/811503800?book_show_action=true">Jan 04, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Danny Tyran" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Danny Tyran" href="/user/show/12973215-danny-tyran">Danny Tyran</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer811503800&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer811503800" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9537038821611622333">This story remembered me why I loved so much reading Heinlein's stories when I was a teenager. The main MCs are endearing. The story has suspense and is not without meaning. On the contrary, I could tell that a youth reading it would be introduced to another way of seeing his/her world. <br/><blockquote>"I used to think of the human race as “lords of creation”-but things had happened to me since. I was not sure that this assemblage would think that humans had rights compared with wormfaces. Certainly the wormfac</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText9537038821611622333" style="display:none">This story remembered me why I loved so much reading Heinlein's stories when I was a teenager. The main MCs are endearing. The story has suspense and is not without meaning. On the contrary, I could tell that a youth reading it would be introduced to another way of seeing his/her world. <br /><blockquote>"I used to think of the human race as “lords of creation”-but things had happened to me since. I was not sure that this assemblage would think that humans had rights compared with wormfaces. Certainly the wormfaces were ahead of us in many ways. When we clear jungle to make farms, do we worry if baboons are there first?"</blockquote><br />Wormfaces are cruel aliens who believe Terreans are not better than bugs.<br /><blockquote>"But when I see a black widow, I step on it; I don’t plead with it to be a good little spider and please stop poisoning people. A black widow spider can’t help it-but that’s the point."</blockquote><br />The author made his research on his subject before writing his story. And in 1958, when this story was published, there was no Internet. You should find books on the subject. And books on space travel were certainly not numerous and the existing few had probably been written by scientists for scientists not for authors of science fiction. It was interesting to see what people knew at the time about space and the possibility of space travels. I realized, that there wasn't even photocopiers at the time, but people knew about relativity law. And their imagination filled up the missing knowledge. What? Heinlein spoke about other dimensions and parallel worlds. In 1958? Scientists are just beginning in 2014 to grapple with the possibility of more than 4 dimensions and of parallel worlds (quantum theory).<br /><br />This book is real science fiction written for the enjoyment of the readers. The story stands up well even today and will surely be read and enjoyed by generations to come. I am sure that many a young person who read this book, yearn to reach the moon in the decades before Armstrong walked on the moon. And it will be the same for the future readers.<br /><br />This book is generally considered as one of Heinlein's juvenile books, because the protagonists are young people: Kip is a high school senior, and Peewee is a girl of about ten; but Heinlein's writing is for all ages. Kip has an urge to go to the moon. The whole beginning of the book shows his cleverness and resourcefulness in figuring how to win a trip to the moon in a contest. He doesn't win it, but he is lucky enough to win a consolation prize: a spacesuit. And he is clever enough to repair it (second-hand spacesuit). Heinlein's description of the many different features of the suit and its reparation is fascinating. And he has a lot to say here about the importance of independence, resourcefulness and initiative. The lesson the book gives is that if you want something, go for it <br /><br />Resigning himself to selling the suit for college tuition money, Kip goes for one last walk; somewhat playfully calling out on the radio, he is surprised to hear an answer to his call. He is amazed when a space ship lands in his backyard and a alien creature comes out and collapses. A second ship lands, an entity gets out and knocks Kip unconscious. The next thing Kip knows he is trapped inside a space ship on his way to the moon, suddenly in the company of a little girl. His captors are "Wormfaces," a species of alien that has been in hiding on the moon, looking at the Earth with evil intentions. Peewee introduces Kip to the "Mother thing," a Vegan entity (and interstellar police person) who radiates love and warmth, effectively communicates with the pair in a bird song type of speech, and inspires undying love and devotion.<br /><br />The book revolves around the two teenagers' attempt to rescue the Mother Thing from the Wormfaces and eventually return to Earth. Before succeeding, they endure captivity on Pluto. Of course, things don't end there, Kip, Oscar, Peewee and the Mother-Thing meet again, then work together to destroy the bad guys (Wormfaces), stare death in the face a few times, and ultimately find themselves representing Earth in an interstellar courtroom before the galactic overlords, the very future of Earth shakily balanced in their own young hands. But as you can imagine, Skip and Peewee save the human race, and with the help of the Mother Thing get home in time for dinner. ;)<br /><br />But Robert Heinlein throws in several sections full of mathematical formulas, high-level theorizing, and advanced scientific concepts. I dare say that these areas of technical speak could turn off some young readers and may well stymie a good number of adults. Aside from the mathematics, Heinlein can make any kind of scientific notions sound feasible and believable, and that is part of his magic and effectiveness. Heinlein's technical forays may be literary speedbumps, but they are interesting none the less. In one scene, Heinlein teaches the reader a mnemonic for memorizing some important facts about our solar system and demonstrates how to solve a problem involving the speed of light mathematically.<br /><br />Most of all, though, Heinlein presents vividly real characters. Since they are youngsters, there are juvenile elements, such as Kip holding back-and-forth conversations with his space suit (whom he names "Oscar") and Peewee, an obnoxious-but-lovable prodigy, always clutching her rag-doll called Madame Pompadour against herself. But these young MCs do interesting, heroic things. <br /><br />Importantly, Heinlein was generations ahead of his time when he made the girls and women in his books as smart and brave as the males. So young readers of both sexes can revel in and be inspired by this book. <br /><br />And I appreciate Heinlein's message on accepting the differences in others, not to only go with "people's" look. Adults who have not yet lost all of their imagination will also relate to the main characters well and delight in a good story line which takes the reader from the earth to the moon to Pluto to another galaxy and back again.<br /><br />As said before the writing is simple, straightforward and easy to follow. Characterization is good. There is a simple but effective suspense. The only little weakness is the length of the scientific and technical explanations. I recommend this book to all the science fiction lovers, but maybe not as your first Heinlein's novel. I rather suggest you to try first: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/350.Stranger_in_a_Strange_Land" title="Stranger in a Strange Land by Robert A. Heinlein" rel="nofollow noopener">Stranger in a Strange Land</a> or <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/348.The_Door_Into_Summer" title="The Door Into Summer by Robert A. Heinlein" rel="nofollow noopener">The Door Into Summer</a>. That's why I give this book 4½ stars, rounded up to 5.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9537038821611622333" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2219574307" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1487667740407249574">3.5 out of 5<br/><br/><i>Have Space Suit - Will Travel</i> is an entertaining, humorous, and optimistic young adult science fiction adventure grounded in a satisfying combination of imagination, believable science, and some interesting social commentary. Liked the characters and loved the scientific explanation and the fact that they occupy such a central role in the story!</span>

          </span>

<div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground">Readers also enjoyed</h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent"><div class='bookCarousel'>
<div class='carouselRow' style='width: 3600px'>
<ul>
<li class='cover' id='bookCover_64396'>
<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/64396.The_Adventures_of_the_Stainless_Steel_Rat"><img alt="The Adventures of the Stainless Steel Rat (Stainless Steel Rat, #4-6)" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/books/1387702198l/64396._SY160_.jpg" />
</a>

  
<script type="text/javascript" charset="utf-8">
//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('rgvZW3fvoDhdidaVq6adgG3IanYgefqU1Q7Tm1Hzoo1VisPf2hA2p2y6bVdszDWouOk1MBsFaFkR5eKhjBo1ZQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Gr0SaWt1e8uHpD7dwYhOTDpfJiWb3It2ou5gi1IAYWjhPAjtxortVLaXhR8G4uZk7355Y6CgGbtmBVGxj+n2gA==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/205.Robert_A_Heinlein">About Robert A. Heinlein</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer14305905253992641788">Robert Anson Heinlein was an American novelist and science fiction writer. Often called "the dean of science fiction writers", he is one of the most popular, influential, and controversial authors of "hard science fiction". <br/><br/>He set a high standard for science and engineering plausibility and helped to raise the genre's standards of literary quality. He was the first SF writer to break into mainstre</span>
  <span id="freeText14305905253992641788" style="display:none">Robert Anson Heinlein was an American novelist and science fiction writer. Often called "the dean of science fiction writers", he is one of the most popular, influential, and controversial authors of "hard science fiction". <br /><br />He set a high standard for science and engineering plausibility and helped to raise the genre's standards of literary quality. He was the first SF writer to break into mainstream, general magazines such as <i>The Saturday Evening Post</i>, in the late 1940s. He was also among the first authors of bestselling, novel-length science fiction in the modern, mass-market era. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14305905253992641788" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
